Looking at the code, I can see:\n\n1. `_get_new_session_key()` method (lines 194-199) - This generates a new session key and checks if it exists. If it doesn't exist, it returns the key. In the cache backend, the create() method does have retry logic with up to 10000 retries. It tries to get a new key and saves with must_create=True. Thread A calls cycle_key()\n   - Saves current data in local variable `data = self._session`\n   - Calls self.create()\n   \n2. create() in db.py:\n   - While loop:\n     - Gets new session key with _get_new_session_key()\n     - Checks if key exists\n     - Saves with must_create=True (force_insert=True)\n     \nThe issue is that between checking exists() and actually saving, another thread could insert the same key!\n\nBUT! The db.py create() method is wrapped in a try-except that catches IntegrityError and converts it to CreateError, which then triggers a retry!\n\nSo the retry should work... Unless...\n\nWait! Let me look at the _get_new_session_key() in base.py again:\n\n```python\ndef _get_new_session_key(self):\n    \"Return session key that isn't being used.\"    while True:\n        session_key = get_random_string(32, VALID_KEY_CHARS)\n        if not self.exists(session_key):\n            return session_key\n```\n\nThis method itself has a while True loop! So if it gets a collision, it just tries again. But what if exists() returns False for a key that already exists in the database?\n\nActually, that's the race condition! Here's the scenario:1. Thread A: _get_new_session_key() generates \"abc123\", checks exists(), gets False (correct)\n2. Thread B: _get_new_session_key() generates \"abc123\", checks exists(), gets False (correct!)\n3. Thread A: Tries to create session with \"abc123\", succeeds\n\n\n4. Thread B: Tries to create session with \"abc123\", gets IntegrityError, raises CreateError\n5. create() catches CreateError and retries, calling _get_new_session_key() again. But here's the problem: _get_new_session_key() could generate the same colliding key again if the timing works out, creating an infinite loop between the two retry mechanisms. The outer create() loop retries the whole operation, but the inner _get_new_session_key() loop just keeps generating keys without any awareness of what create() is doing.\n\nLooking at the actual code structure, create() has a while True loop that calls _get_new_session_key(), then tries to save with must_create=True. When save() fails with CreateError, create() continues the loop and calls _get_new_session_key() again. The issue is that _get_new_session_key() checks exists() to verify uniqueness, but between that check and the actual database insert in save(), another thread could insert the same key, causing the IntegrityError.\n\nIn cycle_key(), the pattern is to get the current session data, call create() to generate a new session, then restore the old data to the cache. This means if create() keeps failing due to key collisions, cycle_key() would be stuck in that loop indefinitely.\n\nThe real problem emerges when considering concurrent requests: if two threads both call cycle_key() simultaneously, they each extract their session data into local variables, then both attempt to create new sessions. If they generate the same key, one will fail with IntegrityError. But the retry logic in create() doesn't account for the fact that the calling code (cycle_key()) has already captured the old data and is waiting to restore it—if the retry succeeds with a different key, the restoration still happens, but the timing and state management becomes fragile.\n\nThe core issue is that data loss occurs when the session key collision happens at just the wrong moment. Looking at the test case being set up, it seems the vulnerability lies in how cycle_key() handles the window between extracting data and restoring it to a new session, especially under the race condition where create() retries with a new key.\n\nIf Thread A's cycle_key() reads the data, then Thread B's create() generates a collision and saves empty data to that same key before Thread A can restore its data, the original session information gets overwritten and lost. The test is checking whether cycle_key() properly loads existing session data before attempting the rotation. The issue could stem from a race condition in _get_new_session_key(). When it checks exists() and finds the key is available, another thread might create that same key before save() is called. If save() encounters an IntegrityError, create() retries by calling _get_new_session_key() again—but if the competing session hasn't been cleaned up yet, it could return the same key. Though actually, exists() should catch that on the retry. Let me re-read the incident description more carefully and think about what the actual race condition might be.\n\nThe incident says:\n- Users report intermittent logouts immediately after logging in\n- The issue is rare under normal load (0.1%) but increases with traffic (up to 2% during peak)\n- Session data is sometimes lost during the login process\n- The issue correlates strongly with concurrency\n\nWhen a user logs in, the session middleware likely calls `session.cycle_key()` to prevent session fixation attacks. During high concurrency, multiple logins could happen simultaneously.\n\nLet me think about the race condition scenario again:\n\nThread A (User Login 1):\n1. Session exists with key \"session_abc\" (empty or from previous request)\n2. User authenticates successfully\n3. Auth data is stored in session: session['user'] = {...}\n4. session.save() is called\n5. session.cycle_key() is called to rotate the key (prevent session fixation)\n   a. data = self._session  (gets {'user': {...}})\n   b. key = self.session_key  (saves \"session_abc\")\n   c. self.create()  (creates new session with key \"session_def\", empty data)\n      - _get_new_session_key() checks exists(\"session_def\"), returns False\n      - saves empty session with key \"session_def\"\n   d. self._session_cache = data  (restores {'user': {...}})\n   e. self.delete(\"session_abc\")  (deletes old session)\n\nThread B (User Login 2):\n- At the same time, running similar operations\n\nThe issue is: when two threads call _get_new_session_key() simultaneously:\n1. Thread A: get_random_string() generates \"session_xyz\"\n2. Thread B: get_random_string() generates \"session_xyz\" (COLLISION!)\n3. Thread A: exists(\"session_xyz\") returns False\n\n\n4. Thread B: exists(\"session_xyz\") returns False (RACE CONDITION!)\n5. Thread A: self._session_key = \"session_xyz\"\n6. Thread B: self._session_key = \"session_xyz\"\n7. Thread A: save() succeeds\n8. Thread B: save() fails with IntegrityError, raises CreateError\n9. Thread B's create() catches CreateError and retries, calling _get_new_session_key() again, which might return the same key if Thread A hasn't saved to the database yet due to transaction isolation issues.\n\nThe database save uses transaction.atomic(), so changes should be immediately visible. Looking at the create() method in db.py, I see the loop keeps calling _get_new_session_key() on each retry after a CreateError. The real problem emerges in cycle_key() from base.py—it captures the old key before calling create(), but if create() fails and retries, the new key assignment happens on the instance variable, creating a mismatch between what was captured and what actually gets used.\n\nIf create() fails multiple times and regenerates the session key repeatedly, the original captured key might not correspond to any actual session in the database. When delete() is called with that stale key, it could delete the wrong session or nothing at all. The code assumes the database constraint will prevent duplicate keys, but there's no guarantee that successive calls to _get_new_session_key() produce unique values—they're random, so collisions are theoretically possible.\n\nThe real vulnerability is a time-of-check-time-of-use race condition: _get_new_session_key() verifies the key doesn't exist, but another thread could insert that same key before save(must_create=True) executes. The retry loop in create() then generates a new key and tries again, but this window of vulnerability persists. Looking at the cache backend implementation, it attempts to create a session up to 10,000 times, calling _get_new_session_key() on each iteration and then trying to save with must_create=True. If the infinite loop in _get_new_session_key() keeps generating the same colliding key, the retry mechanism in create() won't help—it'll just keep hitting the same collision repeatedly.\n\nThe db backend, by contrast, has no retry limit at all, which means it could theoretically loop forever. But the more critical problem is in cycle_key(): it stores the old key in a local variable before calling create(), but if create() fails and retries multiple times, the stored key might not match what actually gets deleted afterward.\n\nLooking at this more carefully, the real issue seems to be the sequence of operations during key rotation. When a user logs in and cycle_key() runs, it extracts the session data, creates a new empty session, then attempts to restore the old data. If something fails between creating the new session and restoring the data—or if the data extraction itself is problematic—the user's authentication information could vanish silently.\n\nThe `_session` property relies on `_get_session()`, which caches the session data in `_session_cache`. Once cached, subsequent calls just return the cached version rather than reloading. This could be problematic if the underlying session data changes between when we capture it and when we try to restore it, or if there's any timing issue in the restoration process.\n\nLooking at this differently—the problem might not be about concurrent access to the same session object, since each request typically gets its own session instance. Instead, the real issue could be in `_get_new_session_key()` itself. With session keys being 32 characters from a 36-character set, collisions should be statistically rare, but under heavy concurrent load with many simultaneous session operations, a race condition becomes possible: Thread A generates a key, checks that it doesn't exist, and gets False. Meanwhile, Thread B generates the same key, also checks and gets False. Both threads then try to save with the same key, causing a collision.\n\nThe trickier part is what happens on retry. After Thread B's save fails with an IntegrityError and it calls `_get_new_session_key()` again, the function could theoretically generate the same colliding key a second time if the exists() check isn't properly reflecting the database state—perhaps due to transaction isolation levels or caching issues. This could create a retry loop. However, this still doesn't directly explain data loss unless the session data itself isn't being persisted correctly during the save operation.\n\nLooking at the actual code flow, the real problem seems to be that `create()` has an unbounded retry loop when collisions occur, and there's no mechanism to prevent regenerating the same keys repeatedly. The safest approach would be to either limit retries in `create()`, modify `_get_new_session_key()` to track previously attempted keys and avoid regenerating them, or ensure the entire operation within `cycle_key()` is atomic so session data doesn't get lost between key generation and persistence. The create() method already has a retry loop that catches collisions and tries again, but _get_new_session_key() has no mechanism to avoid regenerating the same failed keys. I could modify _get_new_session_key() to accept an exclusion list of keys to skip, allowing create() to pass previously failed keys and ensure different attempts. However, since _get_new_session_key() is used elsewhere in the codebase, I need to be careful about changing its signature without breaking other callers.\n\nA safer approach might be adding a retry limit to create() that raises an exception if collisions persist beyond a reasonable threshold. Looking at the cycle_key() method more carefully—it calls create(), then restores the cached session data, then deletes the old key. If create() is retrying internally and setting self._session_key multiple times during those retries, the final delete(key) call might target the wrong session key, leaving orphaned sessions behind.\n\nThe core issue seems to be that when a collision occurs during create(), the new session gets saved with empty data before the restoration happens. If that save operation partially succeeds—deleting the old session but failing to create the new one—the data could be lost between those two operations.\n\nLooking at the save() method more carefully, when must_create=True and an IntegrityError occurs, it raises CreateError immediately. The transaction wrapper should prevent partial success, but the real problem is the sequence: if the collision happens after the old session is already deleted, we've lost the data window. The fix needs to ensure that when a collision is detected, we retry with a completely new key rather than risking data loss in that gap. If `_get_new_session_key()` keeps generating keys that are already in use, the loop has no exit condition and could run indefinitely. There's also a risk of a race condition where the existence check passes but another process creates the same key before this one does, causing an infinite retry cycle.\n\nAdding a maximum retry count would prevent runaway loops. I can see that:1. _key_to_file() calls _get_or_create_session_key(), which could raise CreateError\n2. _key_to_file() is called in load() at line 82 within a try-except for OSError\n3. If CreateError is raised, it won't be caught by the OSError except clause, so it will propagate up\n4. But this is fine because load() is called from _get_session() in base.py, which is called lazily when accessing the session\n5. If CreateError is raised during load(), it will propagate up to the caller, which can handle it appropriately\n\nThe issue I'm concerned about is: what if load() is called before create()? Let me trace through the flow:1. New request comes in, session middleware loads an empty session (no session key yet)\n2. User authenticates successfully\n3. Auth data is stored in session: session['user'] = {...}\n4. session.cycle_key() is called to prevent session fixation\n5. cycle_key() extracts data, calls create()\n6. create() succeeds (hopefully)\n7. cycle_key() restores data\n8. session.save() is called\n\nSo in this flow, create() is called before load() in the context of cycle_key(). So there shouldn't be an issue.But what if load() is called before create()? For example:\n1. New session object created with no session key\n2. session.load() is called (e.g., in some test)\n3. _key_to_file() calls _get_or_create_session_key()\n4. _get_or_create_session_key() calls _get_new_session_key()\n5. _get_new_session_key() returns a key\n6. load() continues\n\nThis should work fine because _get_new_session_key() should succeed on the first try (collisions are very rare).The only issue is if there are 10000+ simultaneous attempts to generate a key, in which case _get_new_session_key() will raise CreateError.
